Frequently asked questions 
| â€¢  4 is sold as a one-time purchase, which means you pay a single, up-front cost to get Office apps for one computer. One-time purchases are available for both PCs and Macs. However, there are no upgrade options, which means if you plan to upgrade to the next major release, youâ€™ll have to buy it at full price.
Microsoft 365 Personal and Microsoft 365 Family are subscriptions that include powerful productivity apps and creativity tools with AI-powered features. In addition to premium desktop versions of popular Microsoft 365 apps like Word, PowerPoint, Excel and Outlook, you also get spacious cloud storage and cloud-connected features that let you collaborate on files in real time. With a subscription, youâ€™ll always have the latest features, fixes and security updates along with ongoing tech support at no extra cost. You can choose to pay for your subscription on a monthly or yearly basis, and use your apps on multiple PCs, Macs, tablets and phones. Additionally, the Microsoft 365 Family plan lets you share your subscription with up to five more people. Everyone gets their own apps and storage. (AI features only available to subscription owner and cannot be shared; AI usage limits apply; minimum age limits may apply to subscription activation and use of AI features. Learn more.)
â€¢  â€¢  Microsoft 365 is compatible with PC, Mac, Android and iOS. See system requirements for compatible versions of your devices, and for other feature requirements. 
â€¢  â€¢  No. Microsoft 365â€™s applications are tailored for each platform and each operating system. The applications available for Mac users and the specific features included may be different from those available for PC users. With Microsoft 365, you can be flexible. With your account, you are not limited to exclusively Mac or exclusively PC, so you can transition across devices. 
â€¢  â€¢  Yes. Documents that you have created belong fully to you. You can choose to store them online on OneDrive or locally on your PC or Mac. 
â€¢  â€¢  Internet access is required to install and activate all the latest releases of apps and services included in all Microsoft 365 subscription plans. Note that if you are an existing subscriber, you do not need to reinstall or purchase another subscription.
For Microsoft 365 plans, internet access is also needed to manage your subscription account, for example to install Office apps on other PCs or to change billing options. Internet access is also required to access documents stored on OneDrive, unless you install the OneDrive desktop app.
You should also connect to the internet regularly to keep your version of Microsoft 365 up to date and to benefit from automatic upgrades. If you do not connect to the internet at least every 31 days, your apps will go into reduced functionality mode, which means that you can view or print your documents but cannot edit the documents or create new ones. To reactivate your apps, simply reconnect to the internet.
You do not need to be connected to the internet to use the Office apps, such as Word, Excel and PowerPoint, because the apps are fully installed on your computer.
â€¢  â€¢  Your Microsoft account is the combination of an email address and password that you use to sign in to services like OneDrive, Xbox LIVE and Outlook.com. If you use any of these services, you already have a Microsoft account that you can use, or you can create a new account. Learn more about Microsoft accounts.
As part of signing up for a trial or purchasing Microsoft 365, you will be prompted to sign in with a Microsoft account. You must be signed in with this account to install and manage your Microsoft 365 subscription, or to use some subscription benefits, including cloud storage.
â€¢  â€¢  You can share Microsoft 365 Family with five other people, for a total of six users. Microsoft 365 Personal can be used by one person. 
â€¢  â€¢  If you have an active Microsoft 365 Family subscription, you can share it with up to five other people. Each person you share your subscription with can install Microsoft 365 on all their devices and sign in to five devices at the same time.
To add someone to your subscription, sign in to your Microsoft account and follow the on-screen instructions to add a user. Each person you add will receive an email with the steps they need to follow. Once they have accepted and completed the steps, their information, including the installs they are using, will appear on their My Account page. You can stop sharing your subscription with someone or remove a device they are using by logging into your Microsoft account.
â€¢  â€¢  Visit learn more about free apps. 
â€¢  â€¢  Microsoft Defender is a cross-device security app that helps individuals and families protect their data and devices by continuously scanning the web for threats to your identity and personal data (US only). Defender also helps you stay safer online with malware protection, real-time security notifications and security tips. Download the Microsoft Defender app. 
â€¢  â€¢  Microsoft Defender is a new cross-device app that helps people and families stay safer online. Microsoft Defender adds new features and a simplified user interface. Microsoft Defender also brings valuable device protection to iOS, Android, Windows and Mac, with malware protection, web protection, real-time security notifications and security tips. Microsoft Defender is available in the Apple, Google and Microsoft app stores and requires a Microsoft 365 Personal or Family subscription to use.
Windows Security, formerly known as Windows Defender Security Centre, is built-in security on Windows PCs to protect your device and data. Windows Security is pre-installed and automatically enabled. Windows Security includes Microsoft Defender Antivirus software that protects your Windows device and data against viruses, ransomware, trojans and other malware unless non-Microsoft antivirus software is active.
â€¢  â€¢  A free in-browser video editing platform designed to make video creation accessible for everyone. 
â€¢  â€¢  AI features included in Microsoft 365 Family plans are only available to the subscription owner and cannot be shared with others. 
â€¢  â€¢  To use Copilot in Word, Excel, PowerPoint, OneNote and Outlook, make sure you have the latest version of Microsoft 365 installed. If you're signed in, have the latest updates installed, and still don't see Copilot, please restart your Microsoft 365 apps. Learn more about why I am not seeing Copilot in my apps. 
â€¢  â€¢  Microsoft 365 supports Arabic, Chinese Simplified, Chinese Traditional, Czech, Danish, Dutch, English, Finnish, French, German, Hebrew, Hungarian, Italian, Japanese, Korean, Norwegian, Polish, Portuguese, Russian, Spanish, Swedish, Thai and Turkish. â€¢  Microsoft 365 Business Basic, Business Standard and Business Premium are tailored for businesses, offering professional email with a custom domain, admin controls for managing access and devices and scalability to add additional users as your business grows. They include advanced security features like Exchange Online Protection to guard against phishing and malware, with Business Premium adding Microsoft Defender for Business for ransomware protection and advanced threat management. Plus, you can access professional collaboration tools like Microsoft Teams with meeting recordings, transcription and team workspaces, while business apps such as Microsoft Bookings can simplify meeting and appointment scheduling. Microsoft Certified: Azure AI Fundamentals 
Overview
This course introduces fundamentals concepts related to artificial intelligence (AI), and the services in Microsoft Azure that can be used to create AI solutions. The course is not designed to teach students to become professional data scientists or software developers, but rather to build awareness of common AI workloads and the ability to identify Azure services to support them. The course is designed as a blended learning experience that combines instructor-led training with online materials on the Microsoft Learn platform (https://azure.com/learn). The hands-on exercises in the course are based on Learn modules, and students are encouraged to use the content on Learn as reference materials to reinforce what they learn in the class and to explore topics in more depth.
Audience Profile
The Azure AI Fundamentals course is designed for anyone interested in learning about the types of solution artificial intelligence (AI) makes possible, and the services on Microsoft Azure that you can use to create them. You donâ€™t need to have any experience of using Microsoft Azure before taking this course, but a basic level of familiarity with computer technology and the Internet is assumed. Some of the concepts covered in the course require a basic understanding of mathematics, such as the ability to interpret charts. The course includes hands-on activities that involve working with data and running code, so a knowledge of fundamental programming principles will be helpful.
Course Syllabus
You can prepare in instructor-led training or self-paced study

â€¢	Install C and C++ support in Visual 
â€¢	Studio 
â€¢	Article â€¢ 12/09/2021 
â€¢	If you haven't downloaded and installed Visual Studio and the Microsoft C/C++ tools 
â€¢	yet, here's how to get started. 
â€¢	Visual Studio 2022 Installation 
â€¢	Welcome to Visual Studio 2022! In this version, it's easy to choose and install just the 
â€¢	features you need. And because of its reduced minimum footprint, it installs quickly and 
â€¢	with less system impact. 
â€¢	ï¼— Note 
â€¢	This topic applies to installation of Visual Studio on Windows. Visual Studio Code 
â€¢	is a lightweight, cross-platform development environment that runs on Windows, 
â€¢	Mac, and Linux systems. The Microsoft C/C++ for Visual Studio Code extension 
â€¢	supports IntelliSense, debugging, code formatting, auto-completion. Visual Studio 
â€¢	for Mac doesn't support Microsoft C++, but does support .NET languages and 
â€¢	cross-platform development. For installation instructions, see Install Visual Studio 
â€¢	for Mac. 
â€¢	Want to know more about what else is new in this version? See the Visual Studio release 
â€¢	notes. 
â€¢	Ready to install? We'll walk you through it, step-by-step. 
â€¢	Step 1 - Make sure your computer is ready for Visual 
â€¢	Studio 
â€¢	Before you begin installing Visual Studio: 
â€¢	1. Check the system requirements. These requirements help you know whether your 
â€¢	computer supports Visual Studio 2022. 
â€¢	2. Apply the latest Windows updates. These updates ensure that your computer has 
â€¢	both the latest security updates and the required system components for Visual 
â€¢	Studio.3. Reboot. The reboot ensures that any pending installs or updates don't hinder the 
â€¢	Visual Studio install. 
â€¢	4. Free up space. Remove unneeded files and applications from your %SystemDrive% 
â€¢	by, for example, running the Disk Cleanup app. 
â€¢	For questions about running previous versions of Visual Studio side by side with Visual 
â€¢	Studio 2022, see the Visual Studio 2022 Platform Targeting and Compatibility page. 
â€¢	Step 2 - Download Visual Studio 
â€¢	Next, download the Visual Studio bootstrapper file. To do so, choose the following 
â€¢	button to go to the Visual Studio download page. Select the edition of Visual Studio that 
â€¢	you want and choose the Free trial or Free download button. 
â€¢	Download Visual Studio 
â€¢	Step 3 - Install the Visual Studio installer 
â€¢	Run the bootstrapper file you downloaded to install the Visual Studio Installer. This new 
â€¢	lightweight installer includes everything you need to both install and customize Visual 
â€¢	Studio. 
â€¢	1. From your Downloads folder, double-click the bootstrapper that matches or is 
â€¢	similar to one of the following files: 
â€¢	vs_community.exe for Visual Studio Community 
â€¢	vs_professional.exe for Visual Studio Professional 
â€¢	vs_enterprise.exe for Visual Studio Enterprise 
â€¢	If you receive a User Account Control notice, choose Yes to allow the bootstrapper 
â€¢	to run. 
â€¢	2. We'll ask you to acknowledge the Microsoft License Terms and the Microsoft 
â€¢	Privacy Statement . Choose Continue. 
â€¢	Step 4 - Choose workloads 
â€¢	After the installer is installed, you can use it to customize your installation by selecting 
â€¢	the workloads, or feature sets, that you want. Here's how. 
â€¢	1. Find the workload you want in the Installing Visual Studio screen.For core C and C++ support, choose the "Desktop development with C++" 
â€¢	workload. It comes with the default core editor, which includes basic code editing 
â€¢	support for over 20 languages, the ability to open and edit code from any folder 
â€¢	without requiring a project, and integrated source code control. 
â€¢	Additional workloads support other kinds of development. For example, choose 
â€¢	the "Universal Windows Platform development" workload to create apps that use 
â€¢	the Windows Runtime for the Microsoft Store. Choose "Game development with 
â€¢	C++" to create games that use DirectX, Unreal, and Cocos2d. Choose "Linux 
â€¢	development with C++" to target Linux platforms, including IoT development. 
â€¢	The Installation details pane lists the included and optional components installed 
â€¢	by each workload. You can select or deselect optional components in this list. For 
â€¢	example, to support development by using the Visual Studio 2017 or 2015 
â€¢	compiler toolsets, choose the MSVC v141 or MSVC v140 optional components. You 
â€¢	can add support for MFC, the experimental Modules language extension, 
â€¢	IncrediBuild, and more. 
â€¢	2. After you choose the workload(s) and optional components you want, choose 
â€¢	Install. 
â€¢	Next, status screens appear that show the progress of your Visual Studio 
â€¢	installation. 
â€¢	îª€ Tip 
â€¢	At any time after installation, you can install workloads or components that you 
â€¢	didn't install initially. If you have Visual Studio open, go to Tools > Get Tools andFeatures... which opens the Visual Studio Installer. Or, open Visual Studio Installer 
â€¢	from the Start menu. From there, you can choose the workloads or components 
â€¢	that you wish to install. Then, choose Modify. 
â€¢	Step 5 - Choose individual components (Optional) 
â€¢	If you don't want to use the Workloads feature to customize your Visual Studio 
â€¢	installation, or you want to add more components than a workload installs, you can do 
â€¢	so by installing or adding individual components from the Individual components tab. 
â€¢	Choose what you want, and then follow the prompts. 
â€¢	Step 6 - Install language packs (Optional) 
â€¢	By default, the installer program tries to match the language of the operating system 
â€¢	when it runs for the first time. To install Visual Studio in a language of your choosing, 
â€¢	choose the Language packs tab from the Visual Studio Installer, and then follow the 
â€¢	prompts. 
â€¢	Change the installer language from the command line 
â€¢	Another way that you can change the default language is by running the installer from 
â€¢	the command line. For example, you can force the installer to run in English by using the 
â€¢	following command: vs_installer.exe --locale en-US . The installer will remember this 
â€¢	setting when it's run the next time. The installer supports the following language tokens: 
â€¢	zh-cn, zh-tw, cs-cz, en-us, es-es, fr-fr, de-de, it-it, ja-jp, ko-kr, pl-pl, pt-br, ru-ru, and tr-tr.Step 7 - Change the installation location (Optional) 
â€¢	You can reduce the installation footprint of Visual Studio on your system drive. You can 
â€¢	choose to move the download cache, shared components, SDKs, and tools to different 
â€¢	drives, and keep Visual Studio on the drive that runs it the fastest. 
â€¢	ï¼‰ Important 
â€¢	You can select a different drive only when you first install Visual Studio. If you've 
â€¢	already installed it and want to change drives, you must uninstall Visual Studio and 
â€¢	then reinstall it. 
â€¢	Step 8 - Start developing 
â€¢	1. After Visual Studio installation is complete, choose the Launch button to get 
â€¢	started developing with Visual Studio. 
â€¢	2. On the start window, choose Create a new project. 
â€¢	3. In the search box, enter the type of app you want to create to see a list of available 
â€¢	templates. The list of templates depends on the workload(s) that you chose during 
â€¢	installation. To see different templates, choose different workloads. 
â€¢	You can also filter your search for a specific programming language by using the 
â€¢	Language drop-down list. You can filter by using the Platform list and the Project 
â€¢	type list, too. 
â€¢	4. Visual Studio opens your new project, and you're ready to code! 
â€¢	When Visual Studio is running, you're ready to continue to the next step. 
â€¢	Next Steps

â€¢	Article â€¢ 06/19/2024 
â€¢	Visual Studio is a powerful developer tool that you can use to complete the entire 
â€¢	development cycle in one place. It's a comprehensive integrated development 
â€¢	environment (IDE) that you can use to write, edit, debug, and build code. Then deploy 
â€¢	your app. Visual Studio includes compilers, code completion tools, source control, 
â€¢	extensions, and many other features to enhance every stage of the software 
â€¢	development process. 
â€¢	With the variety of features and languages support in Visual Studio, you can grow from 
â€¢	writing your first "Hello World" program to developing and deploying apps. For 
â€¢	example, build, debug, and test .NET and C++ apps, edit ASP.NET pages in the web 
â€¢	designer view, develop cross-platform mobile and desktop apps with .NET, or build 
â€¢	responsive Web UIs in C#.

â€¢	Studio to download.

â€¢	high-quality code efficiently and collaboratively. 
â€¢	Workload-based installer - install only what you need

â€¢	one placeMultiple language support - code in C++, C#, JavaScript, TypeScript, Python, and 
â€¢	more

â€¢	Version control integration - collaborate on code with team mates

â€¢	î£  NoCreate a C++ console app project 
â€¢	Article â€¢ 07/06/2023 
â€¢	The usual starting point for a C++ programmer is a "Hello, world!" application that runs 
â€¢	on the command line. That's what you create in Visual Studio in this step. 
â€¢	Prerequisites 
â€¢	Have Visual Studio with the Desktop development with C++ workload installed 
â€¢	and running on your computer. If it's not installed yet, see Install C++ support in 
â€¢	Visual Studio. 
â€¢	Create your app project 
â€¢	Visual Studio uses projects to organize the code for an app, and solutions to organize 
â€¢	your projects. A project contains all the options, configurations, and rules used to build 
â€¢	your apps. It manages the relationship between all the project's files and any external 
â€¢	files. To create your app, first, create a new project and solution. 
â€¢	1. In Visual Studio, open the File menu and choose New > Project to open the Create 
â€¢	a new Project dialog. Select the Console App template that has C++, Windows, 
â€¢	and Console tags, and then choose Next.2. In the Configure your new project dialog, enter HelloWorld in the Project name 
â€¢	edit box. Choose Create to create the project. 
â€¢	Visual Studio creates a new project. It's ready for you to add and edit your source 
â€¢	code. By default, the Console App template provides source code for a "Hello 
â€¢	World" app, like this: 
â€¢	When the code looks like this in the editor, you're ready to go on to the next step 
â€¢	and build your app.I ran into a problem. 
â€¢	Next steps 
â€¢	Build and run a C++ project 
â€¢	Troubleshooting guide 
â€¢	Come here for solutions to common issues when you create your first C++ project. 
â€¢	Create your app project: issues 
â€¢	The New Project dialog should show a Console App template that has C++, Windows, 
â€¢	and Console tags. If you don't see it, there are two possible causes. It might be filtered 
â€¢	out of the list, or it might not be installed. First, check the filter dropdowns at the top of 
â€¢	the list of templates. Set them to C++, Windows, and Console. The C++ Console App 
â€¢	template should appear; otherwise, the Desktop development with C++ workload isn't 
â€¢	installed. 
â€¢	To install Desktop development with C++, you can run the installer right from the New 
â€¢	Project dialog. Choose the Install more tools and features link at the bottom of the 
â€¢	template list to start the installer. If the User Account Control dialog requests 
â€¢	permissions, choose Yes. In the installer, make sure the Desktop development with C++ 
â€¢	workload is checked. Then choose Modify to update your Visual Studio installation. 
â€¢	If another project with the same name already exists, choose another name for your 
â€¢	project. Or, delete the existing project and try again. To delete an existing project, delete 
â€¢	the solution folder (the folder that contains the helloworld.sln file) in File Explorer. 
â€¢	Go back.Build and run a C++ console app 
â€¢	project 
â€¢	Article â€¢ 07/01/2024 
â€¢	In Create a C++ console app project you created a C++ console app project and 
â€¢	entered your code. Now you can build and run it within Visual Studio. Then, run it as a 
â€¢	stand-alone app from the command line. 
â€¢	Prerequisites 
â€¢	Have Visual Studio with the Desktop development with C++ workload installed 
â€¢	and running on your computer. If it's not installed, follow the steps in Install C++ 
â€¢	support in Visual Studio. 
â€¢	Create a "Hello, World!" project. By default, it contains code to print Hello World! . 
â€¢	If you haven't done this step yet, follow the steps in Create a C++ console app 
â€¢	project. 
â€¢	If Visual Studio looks like this, you're ready to build and run your app: 
â€¢	Build and run your code in Visual Studio1. To build your project, from the main menu choose Build > Build Solution. The 
â€¢	Output window shows the results of the build process. 
â€¢	2. To run the code, on the menu bar, choose Debug, Start without debugging. 
â€¢	A console window opens and then runs your app. When you start a console app in 
â€¢	Visual Studio, it runs your code, then prints "Press any key to continue . . ." to give 
â€¢	you a chance to see the output.Congratulations! You created your first "Hello, world!" console app in Visual Studio! 
â€¢	Press a key to dismiss the console window and return to Visual Studio. 
â€¢	I ran into a problem. 
â€¢	Run your code in a command window 
â€¢	Normally, you run console apps at the command prompt, not in Visual Studio. Once 
â€¢	Visual Studio builds your app, you can run it from a command window. Here's how to 
â€¢	find and run your new app in a command prompt window. 
â€¢	1. In Solution Explorer, select the HelloWorld solution (not the HelloWorld project) 
â€¢	and right-click to open the context menu. Choose Open Folder in File Explorer to 
â€¢	open a File Explorer window in the HelloWorld solution folder. 
â€¢	2. In the File Explorer window, open the x64 folder and then the Debug folder. This 
â€¢	folder contains your app, HelloWorld.exe , and debugging files. Hold down the 
â€¢	Shift key and right-click on HelloWorld.exe to open the context menu. Choose 
â€¢	Copy as path to copy the path to your app to the clipboard. If you see 
â€¢	HelloWorld.exe.recipe , it's because you did the Open Folder in File Explorer step 
â€¢	on the HelloWorld project instead of the HelloWorld solution. Navigate up a level in 
â€¢	File Explorer to get to the solution folder. This folder also contains a x64\Debug\ 
â€¢	folder, where HelloWorld.exe is. 
â€¢	3. To open a command prompt window, press Windows+R to open the Run dialog. 
â€¢	Enter cmd.exe in the Open textbox, then choose OK to run a command prompt 
â€¢	window. 
â€¢	4. In the command prompt window, right-click to paste the path to your app into the 
â€¢	command prompt. Press Enter to run your app.Congratulations, you built and ran a console app in Visual Studio! 
â€¢	I ran into a problem. 
â€¢	Next Steps 
â€¢	Once you build and run this simple app, you're ready for more complex projects. For 
â€¢	more information, see Using the Visual Studio IDE for C++ Desktop Development. It has 
â€¢	more detailed walkthroughs that explore the capabilities of Microsoft C++ in Visual 
â€¢	Studio.

â€¢	If red squiggles appear under anything in the source code editor, the build may have 
â€¢	errors or warnings. Check that your code matches the example in spelling, punctuation, 
â€¢	and case. 
â€¢	Go back.

â€¢	If the path shown in File Explorer ends in \HelloWorld\HelloWorld , you opened the 
â€¢	HelloWorld project instead of the HelloWorld solution. You won't see your app in the 
â€¢	x64\Debug folder. Navigate up a level in File Explorer to get to the solution folder, the 
â€¢	first HelloWorld in the path. This folder also contains a x64\Debug folder, where your 
â€¢	app is. 
â€¢	You can also navigate to the solution x64\Debug folder at the command line to run your 
â€¢	app. Your app won't run from other directories without specifying the path to the app. 
â€¢	However, you can copy your app to another directory and run it from there. It's also 
â€¢	possible to copy it to a directory specified by your PATH environment variable, then run 
â€¢	it from anywhere. 
â€¢	If you don't see Copy as path in the shortcut menu, dismiss the menu, and then hold 
â€¢	down the Shift key while you open it again. This command is just for convenience. You 
â€¢	can also copy the path to the folder from the File Explorer search bar, and paste it into 
â€¢	the Run dialog, and then enter the name of your executable at the end. It's just a little 
â€¢	more typing, but it has the same result. 
â€¢	Go back. 
â€¢	î£¡ Yes 
â€¢	î£  NoWelcome back to C++ - Modern C++ 
â€¢	Article â€¢ 11/07/2022 
â€¢	Since its creation, C++ has become one of the most widely used programming 
â€¢	languages in the world. Well-written C++ programs are fast and efficient. The language 
â€¢	is more flexible than other languages: It can work at the highest levels of abstraction, 
â€¢	and down at the level of the silicon. C++ supplies highly optimized standard libraries. It 
â€¢	enables access to low-level hardware features, to maximize speed and minimize memory 
â€¢	requirements. C++ can create almost any kind of program: Games, device drivers, HPC, 
â€¢	cloud, desktop, embedded, and mobile apps, and much more. Even libraries and 
â€¢	compilers for other programming languages get written in C++. 
â€¢	One of the original requirements for C++ was backward compatibility with the C 
â€¢	language. As a result, C++ has always permitted C-style programming, with raw 
â€¢	pointers, arrays, null-terminated character strings, and other features. They may enable 
â€¢	great performance, but can also spawn bugs and complexity. The evolution of C++ has 
â€¢	emphasized features that greatly reduce the need to use C-style idioms. The old C
â€¢	programming facilities are still there when you need them. However, in modern C++ 
â€¢	code you should need them less and less. Modern C++ code is simpler, safer, more 
â€¢	elegant, and still as fast as ever. 
â€¢	The following sections provide an overview of the main features of modern C++. Unless 
â€¢	noted otherwise, the features listed here are available in C++11 and later. In the 
â€¢	Microsoft C++ compiler, you can set the /std compiler option to specify which version 
â€¢	of the standard to use for your project. 
â€¢	Resources and smart pointers 
â€¢	One of the major classes of bugs in C-style programming is the memory leak. Leaks are 
â€¢	often caused by a failure to call delete for memory that was allocated with new . 
â€¢	Modern C++ emphasizes the principle of resource acquisition is initialization (RAII). The 
â€¢	idea is simple. Resources (heap memory, file handles, sockets, and so on) should be 
â€¢	owned by an object. That object creates, or receives, the newly allocated resource in its 
â€¢	constructor, and deletes it in its destructor. The principle of RAII guarantees that all 
â€¢	resources get properly returned to the operating system when the owning object goes 
â€¢	out of scope. 
â€¢	To support easy adoption of RAII principles, the C++ Standard Library provides three 
â€¢	smart pointer types: std::unique_ptr, std::shared_ptr, and std::weak_ptr. A smart pointer 
â€¢	handles the allocation and deletion of the memory it owns. The following exampleshows a class with an array member that is allocated on the heap in the call to 
â€¢	make_unique() . The calls to new and delete are encapsulated by the unique_ptr class. 
â€¢	When a widget object goes out of scope, the unique_ptr destructor will be invoked and 
â€¢	it will release the memory that was allocated for the array. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	Whenever possible, use a smart pointer to manage heap memory. If you must use the 
â€¢	new and delete operators explicitly, follow the principle of RAII. For more information, 
â€¢	see Object lifetime and resource management (RAII). 
â€¢	C-style strings are another major source of bugs. By using std::string and std::wstring, 
â€¢	you can eliminate virtually all the errors associated with C-style strings. You also gain the 
â€¢	benefit of member functions for searching, appending, prepending, and so on. Both are 
â€¢	highly optimized for speed. When passing a string to a function that requires only read
â€¢	only access, in C++17 you can use std::string_view for even greater performance benefit. 
â€¢	The standard library containers all follow the principle of RAII. They provide iterators for 
â€¢	safe traversal of elements. And, they're highly optimized for performance and have been 
â€¢	thoroughly tested for correctness. By using these containers, you eliminate the potential 
â€¢	#include <memory> 
â€¢	class widget 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	private: 
â€¢	std::unique_ptr<int[]> data; 
â€¢	public: 
â€¢	widget(const int size) { data = std::make_unique<int[]>(size); } 
â€¢	void do_something() {} 
â€¢	}; 
â€¢	void functionUsingWidget() { 
â€¢	widget w(1000000); // lifetime automatically tied to enclosing scope 
â€¢	// constructs w, including the w.data gadget member 
â€¢	// ... 
â€¢	w.do_something(); 
â€¢	// ... 
â€¢	} // automatic destruction and deallocation for w and w.data 
â€¢	std::string and std::string_view 
â€¢	std::vector and other Standard Library 
â€¢	containersfor bugs or inefficiencies that might be introduced in custom data structures. Instead of 
â€¢	raw arrays, use vector as a sequential container in C++. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	vector<string> apples; 
â€¢	apples.push_back("Granny Smith"); 
â€¢	Use map (not unordered_map ) as the default associative container. Use set, multimap, 
â€¢	and multiset for degenerate and multi cases. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	map<string, string> apple_color; 
â€¢	// ... 
â€¢	apple_color["Granny Smith"] = "Green"; 
â€¢	When performance optimization is needed, consider using: 
â€¢	Unordered associative containers such as unordered_map. These have lower per
â€¢	element overhead and constant-time lookup, but they can be harder to use 
â€¢	correctly and efficiently. 
â€¢	Sorted vector . For more information, see Algorithms. 
â€¢	Don't use C-style arrays. For older APIs that need direct access to the data, use accessor 
â€¢	methods such as f(vec.data(), vec.size()); instead. For more information about 
â€¢	containers, see C++ Standard Library Containers. 
â€¢	Standard Library algorithms 
â€¢	Before you assume that you need to write a custom algorithm for your program, first 
â€¢	review the C++ Standard Library algorithms. The Standard Library contains an ever
â€¢	growing assortment of algorithms for many common operations such as searching, 
â€¢	sorting, filtering, and randomizing. The math library is extensive. In C++17 and later, 
â€¢	parallel versions of many algorithms are provided. 
â€¢	Here are some important examples: 
â€¢	for_each , the default traversal algorithm (along with range-based for loops). 
â€¢	transform , for not-in-place modification of container elements 
â€¢	find_if , the default search algorithm. 
â€¢	sort , lower_bound , and the other default sorting and searching algorithms.To write a comparator, use strict < and use named lambdas when you can. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	C++11 introduced the auto keyword for use in variable, function, and template 
â€¢	declarations. auto tells the compiler to deduce the type of the object so that you don't 
â€¢	have to type it explicitly. auto is especially useful when the deduced type is a nested 
â€¢	template: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	C-style iteration over arrays and containers is prone to indexing errors and is also 
â€¢	tedious to type. To eliminate these errors, and make your code more readable, use 
â€¢	range-based for loops with both Standard Library containers and raw arrays. For more 
â€¢	information, see Range-based for statement. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	auto comp = [](const widget& w1, const widget& w2) 
â€¢	{ return w1.weight() < w2.weight(); } 
â€¢	sort( v.begin(), v.end(), comp ); 
â€¢	auto i = lower_bound( v.begin(), v.end(), widget{0}, comp ); 
â€¢	auto instead of explicit type names 
â€¢	map<int,list<string>>::iterator i = m.begin(); // C-style 
â€¢	auto i = m.begin(); // modern C++ 
â€¢	Range-based for loops 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	#include <vector> 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	std::vector<int> v {1,2,3}; 
â€¢	// C-style 
â€¢	for(int i = 0; i < v.size(); ++i) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	std::cout << v[i]; 
â€¢	}Macros in C and C++ are tokens that are processed by the preprocessor before 
â€¢	compilation. Each instance of a macro token is replaced with its defined value or 
â€¢	expression before the file is compiled. Macros are commonly used in C-style 
â€¢	programming to define compile-time constant values. However, macros are error-prone 
â€¢	and difficult to debug. In modern C++, you should prefer constexpr variables for 
â€¢	compile-time constants: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	In modern C++, you can use brace initialization for any type. This form of initialization is 
â€¢	especially convenient when initializing arrays, vectors, or other containers. In the 
â€¢	following example, v2 is initialized with three instances of S . v3 is initialized with three 
â€¢	instances of S that are themselves initialized using braces. The compiler infers the type 
â€¢	of each element based on the declared type of v3 . 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	// Modern C++: 
â€¢	for(auto& num : v) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	std::cout << num; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	constexpr expressions instead of macros 
â€¢	#define SIZE 10 // C-style 
â€¢	constexpr int size = 10; // modern C++ 
â€¢	Uniform initialization 
â€¢	#include <vector> 
â€¢	struct S 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	std::string name; 
â€¢	float num; 
â€¢	S(std::string s, float f) : name(s), num(f) {} 
â€¢	}; 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	// C-style initialization 
â€¢	std::vector<S> v; 
â€¢	S s1("Norah", 2.7);For more information, see Brace initialization. 
â€¢	Modern C++ provides move semantics, which make it possible to eliminate unnecessary 
â€¢	memory copies. In earlier versions of the language, copies were unavoidable in certain 
â€¢	situations. A move operation transfers ownership of a resource from one object to the 
â€¢	next without making a copy. Some classes own resources such as heap memory, file 
â€¢	handles, and so on. When you implement a resource-owning class, you can define a 
â€¢	move constructor and move assignment operator for it. The compiler chooses these 
â€¢	special members during overload resolution in situations where a copy isn't needed. The 
â€¢	Standard Library container types invoke the move constructor on objects if one is 
â€¢	defined. For more information, see Move Constructors and Move Assignment Operators 
â€¢	(C++). 
â€¢	In C-style programming, a function can be passed to another function by using a 
â€¢	function pointer. Function pointers are inconvenient to maintain and understand. The 
â€¢	function they refer to may be defined elsewhere in the source code, far away from the 
â€¢	point at which it's invoked. Also, they're not type-safe. Modern C++ provides function 
â€¢	objects, classes that override the operator() operator, which enables them to be called 
â€¢	like a function. The most convenient way to create function objects is with inline lambda 
â€¢	expressions. The following example shows how to use a lambda expression to pass a 
â€¢	function object, that the find_if function will invoke on each element in the vector: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	S s2("Frank", 3.5); 
â€¢	S s3("Jeri", 85.9); 
â€¢	v.push_back(s1); 
â€¢	v.push_back(s2); 
â€¢	v.push_back(s3); 
â€¢	// Modern C++: 
â€¢	std::vector<S> v2 {s1, s2, s3}; 
â€¢	// or... 
â€¢	std::vector<S> v3{ {"Norah", 2.7}, {"Frank", 3.5}, {"Jeri", 85.9} }; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	Move semantics 
â€¢	Lambda expressionsThe lambda expression [=](int i) { return i > x && i < y; } can be read as "function 
â€¢	that takes a single argument of type int and returns a boolean that indicates whether 
â€¢	the argument is greater than x and less than y ." Notice that the variables x and y 
â€¢	from the surrounding context can be used in the lambda. The [=] specifies that those 
â€¢	variables are captured by value; in other words, the lambda expression has its own 
â€¢	copies of those values. 
â€¢	Modern C++ emphasizes exceptions, not error codes, as the best way to report and 
â€¢	handle error conditions. For more information, see Modern C++ best practices for 
â€¢	exceptions and error handling. 
â€¢	Use the C++ Standard Library std::atomic struct and related types for inter-thread 
â€¢	communication mechanisms. 
â€¢	Unions are commonly used in C-style programming to conserve memory by enabling 
â€¢	members of different types to occupy the same memory location. However, unions 
â€¢	aren't type-safe and are prone to programming errors. C++17 introduces the 
â€¢	std::variant class as a more robust and safe alternative to unions. The std::visit function 
â€¢	can be used to access the members of a variant type in a type-safe manner. 
â€¢	C++ Language Reference 
â€¢	Lambda Expressions 
â€¢	C++ Standard Library 
â€¢	Microsoft C/C++ language conformance 
â€¢	std::vector<int> v {1,2,3,4,5}; 
â€¢	int x = 2; 
â€¢	int y = 4; 
â€¢	auto result = find_if(begin(v), end(v), [=](int i) { return i > x && i < 
â€¢	y; }); 
â€¢	Exceptions 
â€¢	std::atomic 
â€¢	std::variant (C++17)

â€¢	Article â€¢ 10/08/2024 
â€¢	The usual starting point for a C++ programmer is a "Hello, world!" application that runs 
â€¢	on the command line. You start with that in this article, and then move on to something 
â€¢	more challenging: a calculator app. 
â€¢	Prerequisites 
â€¢	Visual Studio with the Desktop development with C++ workload installed and 
â€¢	running on your computer. To install it, see Install C++ support in Visual Studio. 
â€¢	This tutorial demonstrates a feature called edit and continue which allows you to 
â€¢	make changes to your code while the app is running. To enable edit and continue, 
â€¢	from the main menu select Tools > Options > Debugging > General and ensure 
â€¢	that Require source files to exactly match the original version is checked. 
â€¢	Create your app project 
â€¢	Visual Studio uses projects to organize the code for an app, and solutions to organize 
â€¢	one or more projects. A project contains all the options, configurations, and rules used 
â€¢	to build an app. It also manages the relationship between all the project's files and any 
â€¢	external files. To create your app, first, create a new project and solution. 
â€¢	1. Start Visual Studio--the Visual Studio Start dialog box appears. Select Create a new 
â€¢	project to get started.2. In the Create a new project dialog, set the language dropdown to C++, set the 
â€¢	platform dropdown to Windows, select Console App from the list of project types, 
â€¢	then select Next. 
â€¢	ï¼‰ Important 
â€¢	Make sure you select the C++ version of the Console App template. It has the 
â€¢	C++, Windows, and Console tags, and the icon has "++" in the corner.3. In the Configure your new project dialog box, select the Project name text box, 
â€¢	name your new project CalculatorTutorial, then select Create. 
â€¢	An empty C++ Windows console application 'Hello World' app is created. Console 
â€¢	applications use a Windows console window to display output and accept user 
â€¢	input. In Visual Studio, an editor window opens and shows the generated code: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	// CalculatorTutorial.cpp : This file contains the 'main' function. 
â€¢	Program execution begins and ends there. 
â€¢	// 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	std::cout << "Hello World!\n"; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	// Run program: Ctrl + F5 or Debug > Start Without Debugging menu 
â€¢	// Debug program: F5 or Debug > Start Debugging menu

â€¢	The template for a new Windows console application creates a simple C++ "Hello 
â€¢	World" app. At this point, you can see how Visual Studio builds and runs the apps you 
â€¢	create right from the IDE. 
â€¢	1. To build your project, select Build Solution from the Build menu. The Output 
â€¢	window shows the results of the build process. 
â€¢	2. To run the code, on the menu bar, select Debug > Start without debugging 
â€¢	(Ctrl+F5).A console window opens and your app runs within it. 
â€¢	When you start a console app in Visual Studio, it runs your code, then prints "Press 
â€¢	any key to close this window . . ." to give you a chance to see the output. 
â€¢	Congratulations! You created your first "Hello, world!" console app in Visual Studio! 
â€¢	3. Press a key to dismiss the console window and return to Visual Studio. 
â€¢	You now have the tools to build and run your app after every change, to verify that the 
â€¢	code still works as you expect. Later, we show you how to debug it if it doesn't. 
â€¢	Now let's modify the code in this template to be a calculator app. 
â€¢	1. Replace the contents of the CalculatorTutorial.cpp file with the following code so 
â€¢	that it matches this example: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	Edit the code 
â€¢	// CalculatorTutorial.cpp : This file contains the 'main' function. 
â€¢	Program execution begins and ends there. 
â€¢	// 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	using namespace std; 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	cout << "Calculator Console Application" << endl << endl; 
â€¢	cout << "Please enter the operation to perform. Format: a+b | a-b |Understanding the code: 
â€¢	The #include statement brings in code in other files. Sometimes, you 
â€¢	may see a filename surrounded by angle brackets like <iostream> . The 
â€¢	angle brackets instruct the compiler to look for the iostream header file 
â€¢	first in the standard system directories, and if not found, to look in 
â€¢	directories specific to the project. Other times, you may see a filename 
â€¢	surrounded by quotes like "someHeader.h" . The quotes instruct the 
â€¢	compiler to skip looking in the standard system directories and instead 
â€¢	only look in directories specific to the project. 
â€¢	The using namespace std; tells the compiler to expect code from the 
â€¢	C++ Standard Library to be used in this file. Without this line, each 
â€¢	keyword from the library would have to be preceded with std:: to 
â€¢	denote its scope. For instance, without that line, each reference to cout 
â€¢	would be written as std::cout . The using statement is added to make it 
â€¢	more convenient to access code in another namespace. 
â€¢	The cout keyword is used to print to standard output in C++. The << 
â€¢	operator tells the compiler to send whatever is to the right of it to the 
â€¢	standard output. 
â€¢	The endl keyword is like the Enter key; it ends the line and moves the 
â€¢	cursor to the next line. It's a better practice to put a \n inside the string 
â€¢	(contained by "" ) to do the same thing because endl always flushes the 
â€¢	buffer which can hurt the performance of the program. But since this is a 
â€¢	very small app, endl is used instead. 
â€¢	All C++ statements must end with semicolons and all C++ applications 
â€¢	must contain a main() function. This function is what the program runs at 
â€¢	a*b | a/b" 
â€¢	<< endl; 
â€¢	return 0; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	// Run program: Ctrl + F5 or Debug > Start Without Debugging menu 
â€¢	// Debug program: F5 or Debug > Start Debugging menu 
â€¢	// Tips for Getting Started: 
â€¢	// 1. Use the Solution Explorer window to add/manage files 
â€¢	// 2. Use the Team Explorer window to connect to source control

â€¢	2. To save the file, press Ctrl+S, or select the floppy disk icon in the toolbar under the

â€¢	Without Debugging. You should see a console window appear that looks like this.

â€¢	Add code to do some math 
â€¢	A class is like a blueprint for an object that does something. In this case, we define a 
â€¢	calculator class to contain the math logic. 
â€¢	Add a Calculator class 
â€¢	1. Go to the Project menu and select Add Class. In the Class Name edit box, enter 
â€¢	Calculator. Select OK. 
â€¢	Two new files get added to your project. To save all your changed files at once, 
â€¢	press Ctrl+Shift+S. It's a keyboard shortcut for File > Save All. There's also a 
â€¢	toolbar button for Save All, an icon of two floppy disks, found beside the Savebutton. In general, it's good practice to do Save All frequently, so you don't miss 
â€¢	saving any changes. 
â€¢	The Add Class wizard creates .h and .cpp files that have the same name as the 
â€¢	class. You can see a full list of your project files in the Solution Explorer window, 
â€¢	visible on the side of the IDE. If the window isn't visible, open it from the menu bar 
â€¢	via View > Solution Explorer. 
â€¢	You can open a file by double-clicking it in the Solution Explorer window. Double
â€¢	click Calculator.h to open it. 
â€¢	2. Replace the contents of Calculator.h with the following code so that the file now 
â€¢	looks like this: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	Understanding the code 
â€¢	This code declares a new function called Calculate , which handles math 
â€¢	operations for addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division. 
â€¢	#pragma once 
â€¢	class Calculator 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	public: 
â€¢	double Calculate(double x, char oper, double y); 
â€¢	};C++ code is organized into header ( .h ) files and source ( .cpp ) files. 
â€¢	Some other file extensions are supported by various compilers, but these 
â€¢	are the main ones to know about. Functions and variables are normally 
â€¢	declared, that is, given a name and a type, in header files, and 
â€¢	implemented, or given a definition, in source files. To access code defined 
â€¢	in another file, you can use #include "filename.h" , where filename.h is 
â€¢	the name of the file that declares the variables or functions you want to 
â€¢	use. 
â€¢	It's good practice to organize your code into different files based on what 
â€¢	it does, so it's easy to find the code you need later. In our case, we define 
â€¢	the Calculator class separately from the file containing the main() 
â€¢	function, but we plan to reference the Calculator class in main() . 
â€¢	3. A green squiggle appears under Calculate because although the Calculate 
â€¢	function is declared, it isn't defined. Hover over Calculate , click the down arrow on 
â€¢	the screwdriver icon, and select Create definition of 'Calculate' in Calculator.cpp . 
â€¢	This code is added to Calculator.cpp :Currently, it just returns 0.0. Let's change that. 
â€¢	4. Switch to the Calculator.cpp file in the editor window. Replace the contents of 
â€¢	Calculator::Calculate(double x, char oper, double y) with: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	Understanding the code 
â€¢	The function Calculate takes a number, an operator, and a second 
â€¢	number. Then it performs the requested operation on the two numbers. 
â€¢	The switch statement checks which operator was provided, and executes 
â€¢	the case corresponding to that operation. The default: case is a fallback 
â€¢	double Calculator::Calculate(double x, char oper, double y) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	switch(oper) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	case '+': 
â€¢	return x + y; 
â€¢	case '-': 
â€¢	return x - y; 
â€¢	case '*': 
â€¢	return x * y; 
â€¢	case '/': 
â€¢	return x / y; 
â€¢	default: 
â€¢	return 0.0; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	}in case the user types an operator that isn't handled by any of the 
â€¢	preceding case statements. It's best to handle invalid user input in a 
â€¢	more elegant way, but this is beyond the scope of this tutorial. 
â€¢	The double keyword denotes a type of number that supports decimals. 
â€¢	This type of number is called a floating-point number, and double means 
â€¢	a floating point number that has extra precision. This way, the calculator 
â€¢	can handle both decimal math and integer math. The Calculate function 
â€¢	is required to always return a double-precision floating point number due 
â€¢	to the double at the start of the code (this denotes the function's return 
â€¢	type), which is why we return 0.0 in the default case. 
â€¢	The .h file declares the function prototype, which tells the compiler 
â€¢	upfront what parameters it requires, and what return type to expect from 
â€¢	it. The .cpp file has all the implementation details of the function. 
â€¢	If you build and run the code again at this point, it immediately exits after asking which 
â€¢	operation to perform. So, modify the main function to do multiple calculations. 
â€¢	1. Update the main function in CalculatorTutorial.cpp as follows: 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	Call the Calculator class member functions 
â€¢	// CalculatorTutorial.cpp : This file contains the 'main' function. 
â€¢	Program execution begins and ends there. 
â€¢	// 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	#include "Calculator.h" 
â€¢	using namespace std; 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	double x = 0.0; 
â€¢	double y = 0.0; 
â€¢	double result = 0.0; 
â€¢	char oper = '+'; 
â€¢	cout << "Calculator Console Application" << endl << endl; 
â€¢	cout << "Please enter the operation to perform. Format: a+b | a-b | 
â€¢	a*b | a/b" 
â€¢	<< endl; 
â€¢	Calculator c; 
â€¢	while (true) 
â€¢	{Understanding the code 
â€¢	Since C++ programs always start at the main() function, we need to call 
â€¢	our other code from there, so an #include statement is needed to make 
â€¢	that code visible to our main() function. 
â€¢	The variables x , y , oper , and result are declared to store the first 
â€¢	number, second number, operator, and final result, respectively. It's 
â€¢	always good practice to give them some initial values to avoid undefined 
â€¢	behavior, which is what is done here. 
â€¢	The Calculator c; line declares an object named c as an instance of the 
â€¢	Calculator class. The class itself is just a blueprint for how calculators 
â€¢	work; the object is the specific calculator that does the math. 
â€¢	The while (true) statement is a loop. The code inside the loop executes 
â€¢	over and over again as long as the condition inside the () holds true. 
â€¢	Since the condition is simply listed as true , it's always true, so the loop 
â€¢	runs forever. To close the program, the user must manually close the 
â€¢	console window. Otherwise, the program always waits for new input. 
â€¢	The cin keyword accepts input from the user. The input stream is smart 
â€¢	enough to process a line of text entered in the console window and place 
â€¢	it inside each of the variables listed, in order. 
â€¢	The c.Calculate(x, oper, y); expression calls the Calculate function 
â€¢	defined earlier, and supplies the entered input values and the requested 
â€¢	operation. The function then returns a number that is stored in result . 
â€¢	Finally, result is printed to the console and the user sees the result of 
â€¢	the calculation. 
â€¢	Now test the program again to make sure everything works properly. 
â€¢	1. Press Ctrl+F5 to rebuild and start the app. 
â€¢	2. Enter 5+5 , and press Enter. Verify that the result is 10. 
â€¢	cin >> x >> oper >> y; 
â€¢	result = c.Calculate(x, oper, y); 
â€¢	cout << "Result " << "of " << x << oper << y << " is: " << 
â€¢	result << endl; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	return 0; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	Build and test the code again3. Stop the program by closing the console window. 
â€¢	Debug the app 
â€¢	Since the user is free to type anything into the console window, let's make sure the 
â€¢	calculator handles unexpected input. Instead of running the program, let's debug it so 
â€¢	we can inspect what it's doing step-by-step. 
â€¢	Run the app in the debugger 
â€¢	1. In CalcuatorTutorial.cpp , set a breakpoint on the line: result = c.Calculate(x, 
â€¢	oper, y); . To set the breakpoint, click next to the line in the gray vertical bar along 
â€¢	the left edge of the editor window so that a red dot appears. 
â€¢	Now when we debug the program, execution pauses at that line. We already have 
â€¢	a rough idea that the program works for simple cases. Since we don't want to 
â€¢	pause execution every time we call Calculate() , let's make the breakpoint 
â€¢	conditional.2. Right-click the red dot that represents the breakpoint, and select Conditions. In 
â€¢	the edit box for the condition, enter (y == 0) && (oper == '/') . Select the Close 
â€¢	button to save the breakpoint condition. 
â€¢	Now, execution pauses at the breakpoint when the app tries to divide by 0. 
â€¢	3. To debug the program, press F5, or select the Local Windows Debugger debugger 
â€¢	toolbar button that has the green arrow icon. In your console app, if you enter 
â€¢	something like "5 - 0", the program behaves normally and keeps running. 
â€¢	However, if you type "10 / 0", it pauses at the breakpoint. You can put any number 
â€¢	of spaces between the operator and numbers: cin is smart enough to parse the 
â€¢	input appropriately.Useful windows in the debugger 
â€¢	When you debug your code, you may notice that some new windows appear. These 
â€¢	windows can assist your debugging experience. Take a look at the Autos window. The 
â€¢	Autos window shows you the current values of variables used at least three lines before 
â€¢	and up to the current line. If you don't see the Autos window, from the main menu 
â€¢	select Debug > Windows > Autos. 
â€¢	To see all of the variables from that function, switch to the Locals window. Because this 
â€¢	is a small function, the Autos and Locals window show the same variables. But you can 
â€¢	modify the values of these variables in the Locals window while debugging to see what 
â€¢	effect they would have on the program. In this case, we leave them alone. Open the 
â€¢	Locals window by selecting Locals at the bottom of the Autos window, or by selecting 
â€¢	from the main menu Debug > Windows > Locals.You can also hover over variables in the code to see their current values at the point 
â€¢	where execution is currently paused. Make sure the editor window is in focus by clicking 
â€¢	on it first. 
â€¢	Continue debugging 
â€¢	1. The yellow arrow on the left shows the current point of execution. The current line 
â€¢	calls Calculate , so press F11 to Step Into the function. Now you're executing code 
â€¢	in the body of the Calculate function. Be careful with Step Into because it steps 
â€¢	into any functions on the line you're on, including standard library functions. It's 
â€¢	fine to step into the standard library, but you may be more interested in focusing 
â€¢	on your code instead of library code. 
â€¢	2. Now that the point of execution is at the start of the Calculate function, press F10 
â€¢	to move to the next line in the program's execution. F10 is also known as Step 
â€¢	Over. You can use Step Over to move from line to line, without delving into the 
â€¢	details of what is occurring in each part of the line. In general, you should use Step 
â€¢	Over instead of Step Into unless you want to dive more deeply into code that is 
â€¢	being called from elsewhere (as you did to reach the body of Calculate ). 
â€¢	3. Continue using F10 to Step Over each line until you get back to the main() 
â€¢	function in the other file, and stop on the cout line.The program is doing what's expected: it takes the first number, and divides it by 
â€¢	the second. On the cout line, hover over the result variable or take a look at 
â€¢	result in the Autos window. Its value is inf , which doesn't look right. 
â€¢	Let's fix it. The cout line outputs whatever value is stored in result , so when you 
â€¢	step one more line forward using F10, the console window displays: 
â€¢	This result is because division by zero is undefined, so the program doesn't have a 
â€¢	numerical answer for the requested operation. 
â€¢	Fix the "divide by zero" error 
â€¢	Let's handle division by zero more gracefully so that it's easier for the user to 
â€¢	understand the problem. 
â€¢	1. Make the following changes in CalculatorTutorial.cpp . You can leave the 
â€¢	program running as you edit, thanks to a debugger feature called Edit andContinue. Add an if statement following cin >> x >> oper >> y; to check for 
â€¢	division by zero and output a message to the user if it happens. Otherwise, the 
â€¢	result is printed. 
â€¢	C++ 
â€¢	2. Press F5 once. Program execution continues until it has to pause to ask for user 
â€¢	input. Enter 10 / 0 again. Now, a more helpful message is printed. The user is 
â€¢	asked for more input, and the program continues executing normally. 
â€¢	// CalculatorTutorial.cpp : This file contains the 'main' function. 
â€¢	Program execution begins and ends there. 
â€¢	// 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	#include "Calculator.h" 
â€¢	using namespace std; 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	double x = 0.0; 
â€¢	double y = 0.0; 
â€¢	double result = 0.0; 
â€¢	char oper = '+'; 
â€¢	cout << "Calculator Console Application" << endl << endl; 
â€¢	cout << "Please enter the operation to perform. Format: a+b | a-b | 
â€¢	a*b | a/b" << endl; 
â€¢	Calculator c; 
â€¢	while (true) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	cin >> x >> oper >> y; 
â€¢	if (oper == '/' && y == 0) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	cout << "Math error: Attempted to divide by zero!" << endl; 
â€¢	continue; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	else 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	result = c.Calculate(x, oper, y); 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	cout << "Result " << "of " << x << oper << y << " is: " << 
â€¢	result << endl; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	return 0; 
â€¢	}ï¼— Note 
â€¢	When you edit code while in debugging mode, there's a risk of code 
â€¢	becoming stale. This happens when the debugger is still running your old 
â€¢	code, and has not yet updated it with your changes. The debugger displays a 
â€¢	dialog to inform you when this happens. Sometimes, you may need to press 
â€¢	F5 to refresh the code being executed. In particular, if you make a change 
â€¢	inside a function while the point of execution is inside that function, you need 
â€¢	to step out of the function, then back into it again to get the updated code. If 
â€¢	that doesn't work and you see an error message, you can stop debugging by 
â€¢	clicking on the red square in the toolbar under the menus at the top of the 
â€¢	IDE, then start debugging again by entering F5 or by choosing the green 
â€¢	"play" arrow beside the stop button on the toolbar. 
â€¢	Another reason edit and continue may fail is if you see a message that says 
â€¢	"The Require source files to exactly match the original version setting under 
â€¢	Debug->Options->General needs to be enabled..." To fix this, from the main 
â€¢	menu select Tools > Options > Debugging > General and ensure that 
â€¢	Require source files to exactly match the original version is checked. 
â€¢	Understanding the Run and Debug shortcuts 
â€¢	F5, or Debug > Start Debugging, starts a debugging session, if one isn't 
â€¢	already active, and runs the program until a breakpoint is hit or the 
â€¢	program needs user input. If no user input is needed and no breakpoint 
â€¢	is available to hit, the program terminates and the console window closes 
â€¢	itself when the program finishes running. If your program outputs to the 
â€¢	console, use Ctrl+F5 or set a breakpoint before you press F5 to keep the 
â€¢	window open. 
â€¢	Ctrl+F5, or Debug > Start Without Debugging, runs the application 
â€¢	without going into debug mode. This is slightly faster than debugging, 
â€¢	and the console window stays open after the program finishes executing. 
â€¢	F10, known as Step Over, lets you iterate through code, line-by-line, and 
â€¢	visualize how the code is run and what variable values are at each step of 
â€¢	execution.F11, known as Step Into, works similarly to Step Over, except it steps into 
â€¢	any functions called on the line of execution. For example, if the line 
â€¢	being executed calls a function, pressing F11 moves the pointer into the 
â€¢	body of the function, so you can follow the function's code being run 
â€¢	before coming back to the line you started at. Pressing F10 steps over the 
â€¢	function call and just moves to the next line; the function call still 
â€¢	happens, but the program doesn't pause to show you what it's doing. 
â€¢	Close the app 
â€¢	If it's still running, close the console window to stop the calculator app. 
â€¢	Add Git source control 
â€¢	Now that you've created an app, you might want to add it to a Git repository. We've got 
â€¢	you covered. Visual Studio makes that process easy with Git tools you can use directly 
â€¢	from the IDE. 
â€¢	îª€ Tip 
â€¢	Git is the most widely used modern version control system, so whether you're a 
â€¢	professional developer or you're learning how to code, Git can be very useful. If 
â€¢	you're new to Git, the https://git-scm.com/ website is a good place to start. 
â€¢	There, you can find cheat sheets, a popular online book, and Git Basics videos. 
â€¢	To associate your code with Git, you start by creating a new Git repository where your 
â€¢	code is located. Here's how: 
â€¢	1. In the status bar at the bottom-right corner of Visual Studio, select Add to Source 
â€¢	Control, and then select Git. 
â€¢	2. In the Create a Git repository dialog box, sign in to GitHub.The repository name auto-populates based on your folder location. By default, 
â€¢	your new repository is private, which means you're the only one who can access it. 
â€¢	îª€ Tip 
â€¢	Whether your repository is public or private, it's best to have a remote backup 
â€¢	of your code stored securely on GitHub. Even if you aren't working with a 
â€¢	team, a remote repository makes your code available to you from any 
â€¢	computer. 
â€¢	3. Select Create and Push. 
â€¢	After you create your repository, status details appear in the status bar. 
â€¢	The first icon with the arrows shows how many outgoing/incoming commits are in 
â€¢	your current branch. You can use this icon to pull any incoming commits or push 
â€¢	any outgoing commits. You can also choose to view these commits first. To do so, 
â€¢	select the icon, and then select View Outgoing/Incoming.Feedback 
â€¢	Was this page helpful? 
â€¢	Provide product feedback 
â€¢	| Get help at Microsoft Q&A 
â€¢	The second icon with the pencil shows the number of uncommitted changes to 
â€¢	your code. You can select this icon to view those changes in the Git Changes 
â€¢	window. 
â€¢	To learn more about how to use Git with your app, see the Visual Studio version control 
â€¢	documentation. 
â€¢	Congratulations! You completed the code for the calculator app, built and debugged it, 
â€¢	and added it to a repo, all in Visual Studio. 
â€¢	Learn more about Visual Studio for C++ 
â€¢	The finished app 
â€¢	Next steps 
â€¢	î£¡ Yes 
â€¢	î£  NoGet started with C++/WinRT 
â€¢	Article â€¢ 02/13/2023 
â€¢	ï¼‰ Important 
â€¢	For info about setting up Visual Studio for C++/WinRT developmentâ€”including 
â€¢	installing and using the C++/WinRT Visual Studio Extension (VSIX) and the NuGet 
â€¢	package (which together provide project template and build support)â€”see Visual 
â€¢	Studio support for C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	To get you up to speed with using C++/WinRT, this topic walks through a simple code 
â€¢	example based on a new Windows Console Application (C++/WinRT) project. This 
â€¢	topic also shows how to add C++/WinRT support to a Windows Desktop application 
â€¢	project. 
â€¢	ï¼— Note 
â€¢	While we recommend that you develop with the latest versions of Visual Studio and 
â€¢	the Windows SDK, if you're using Visual Studio 2017 (version 15.8.0 or later), and 
â€¢	targeting the Windows SDK version 10.0.17134.0 (Windows 10, version 1803), then 
â€¢	a newly created C++/WinRT project may fail to compile with the error "error C3861: 
â€¢	'from_abi': identifier not found", and with other errors originating in base.h. The 
â€¢	solution is to either target a later (more conformant) version of the Windows SDK, 
â€¢	or set project property C/C++ > Language > Conformance mode: No (also, if 
â€¢	/permissive- appears in project property C/C++ > Language > Command Line 
â€¢	under Additional Options, then delete it). 
â€¢	A C++/WinRT quick-start 
â€¢	Create a new Windows Console Application (C++/WinRT) project. 
â€¢	Edit pch.h and main.cpp to look like this. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	// pch.h 
â€¢	#pragma once 
â€¢	#include <winrt/Windows.Foundation.Collections.h>C++/WinRT 
â€¢	Let's take the short code example above piece by piece, and explain what's going on in 
â€¢	each part. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	With the default project settings, the included headers come from the Windows SDK, 
â€¢	inside the folder 
â€¢	%WindowsSdkDir%Include<WindowsTargetPlatformVersion>\cppwinrt\winrt . Visual Studio 
â€¢	includes that path in its IncludePath macro. But there's no strict dependency on the 
â€¢	#include <winrt/Windows.Web.Syndication.h> 
â€¢	#include <iostream> 
â€¢	// main.cpp 
â€¢	#include "pch.h" 
â€¢	using namespace winrt; 
â€¢	using namespace Windows::Foundation; 
â€¢	using namespace Windows::Web::Syndication; 
â€¢	int main() 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	winrt::init_apartment(); 
â€¢	Uri rssFeedUri{ L"https://blogs.windows.com/feed" }; 
â€¢	SyndicationClient syndicationClient; 
â€¢	syndicationClient.SetRequestHeader(L"User-Agent", L"Mozilla/5.0 
â€¢	(compatible; MSIE 10.0; Windows NT 6.2; WOW64; Trident/6.0)"); 
â€¢	SyndicationFeed syndicationFeed = 
â€¢	syndicationClient.RetrieveFeedAsync(rssFeedUri).get(); 
â€¢	for (const SyndicationItem syndicationItem : syndicationFeed.Items()) 
â€¢	{ 
â€¢	winrt::hstring titleAsHstring = syndicationItem.Title().Text(); 
â€¢	// A workaround to remove the trademark symbol from the title 
â€¢	string, because it causes issues in this case. 
â€¢	std::wstring titleAsStdWstring{ titleAsHstring.c_str() }; 
â€¢	titleAsStdWstring.erase(remove(titleAsStdWstring.begin(), 
â€¢	titleAsStdWstring.end(), L'â„¢'), titleAsStdWstring.end()); 
â€¢	titleAsHstring = titleAsStdWstring; 
â€¢	std::wcout << titleAsHstring.c_str() << std::endl; 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	#include <winrt/Windows.Foundation.Collections.h> 
â€¢	#include <winrt/Windows.Web.Syndication.h>Windows SDK, because your project (via the cppwinrt.exe tool) generates those same 
â€¢	headers into your project's $(GeneratedFilesDir) folder. They'll be loaded from that 
â€¢	folder if they can't be found elsewhere, or if you change your project settings. 
â€¢	The headers contain Windows APIs projected into C++/WinRT. In other words, for each 
â€¢	Windows type, C++/WinRT defines a C++-friendly equivalent (called the projected type). 
â€¢	A projected type has the same fully-qualified name as the Windows type, but it's placed 
â€¢	in the C++ winrt namespace. Putting these includes in your precompiled header 
â€¢	reduces incremental build times. 
â€¢	ï¼‰ Important 
â€¢	Whenever you want to use a type from a Windows namespaces, you must #include 
â€¢	the corresponding C++/WinRT Windows namespace header file, as shown above. 
â€¢	The corresponding header is the one with the same name as the type's namespace. 
â€¢	For example, to use the C++/WinRT projection for the 
â€¢	Windows::Foundation::Collections::PropertySet runtime class, include the 
â€¢	winrt/Windows.Foundation.Collections.h header. 
â€¢	It is common for a C++/WinRT projection header to automatically include related 
â€¢	namespace header files. For example, winrt/Windows.Foundation.Collections.h 
â€¢	includes winrt/Windows.Foundation.h . But you shouldn't rely on this behavior, since 
â€¢	it's an implementation detail that changes over time. You must explicitly include 
â€¢	any headers that you need. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	using namespace winrt; 
â€¢	using namespace Windows::Foundation; 
â€¢	using namespace Windows::Web::Syndication; 
â€¢	The using namespace directives are optional, but convenient. The pattern shown above 
â€¢	for such directives (allowing unqualified name lookup for anything in the winrt 
â€¢	namespace) is suitable for when you're beginning a new project and C++/WinRT is the 
â€¢	only language projection you're using inside of that project. If, on the other hand, you're 
â€¢	mixing C++/WinRT code with C++/CX and/or SDK application binary interface (ABI) 
â€¢	code (you're either porting from, or interoperating with, one or both of those models), 
â€¢	then see the topics Interop between C++/WinRT and C++/CX, Move to C++/WinRT 
â€¢	from C++/CX, and Interop between C++/WinRT and the ABI. 
â€¢	C++/WinRTwinrt::init_apartment(); 
â€¢	The call to winrt::init_apartment initializes the thread in the Windows Runtime; by 
â€¢	default, in a multithreaded apartment. The call also initializes COM. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	Uri rssFeedUri{ L"https://blogs.windows.com/feed" }; 
â€¢	SyndicationClient syndicationClient; 
â€¢	Stack-allocate two objects: they represent the uri of the Windows blog, and a 
â€¢	syndication client. We construct the uri with a simple wide string literal (see String 
â€¢	handling in C++/WinRT for more ways you can work with strings). 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	SyndicationFeed syndicationFeed = 
â€¢	syndicationClient.RetrieveFeedAsync(rssFeedUri).get(); 
â€¢	SyndicationClient::RetrieveFeedAsync is an example of an asynchronous Windows 
â€¢	Runtime function. The code example receives an asynchronous operation object from 
â€¢	RetrieveFeedAsync, and it calls get on that object to block the calling thread and wait 
â€¢	for the result (which is a syndication feed, in this case). For more about concurrency, and 
â€¢	for non-blocking techniques, see Concurrency and asynchronous operations with 
â€¢	C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	for (const SyndicationItem syndicationItem : syndicationFeed.Items()) { ... 
â€¢	} 
â€¢	SyndicationFeed.Items is a range, defined by the iterators returned from begin and end 
â€¢	functions (or their constant, reverse, and constant-reverse variants). Because of this, you 
â€¢	can enumerate Items with either a range-based for statement, or with the std::for_each 
â€¢	template function. Whenever you iterate over a Windows Runtime collection like this, 
â€¢	you'll need to #include <winrt/Windows.Foundation.Collections.h> . 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	winrt::hstring titleAsHstring = syndicationItem.Title().Text(); 
â€¢	// Omitted: there's a little bit of extra work here to remove the trademark 
â€¢	symbol from the title text.std::wcout << titleAsHstring.c_str() << std::endl; 
â€¢	Gets the feed's title text, as a winrt::hstring object (more details in String handling in 
â€¢	C++/WinRT). The hstring is then output, via the c_str function, which reflects the pattern 
â€¢	used with C++ Standard Library strings. 
â€¢	As you can see, C++/WinRT encourages modern, and class-like, C++ expressions such 
â€¢	as syndicationItem.Title().Text() . This is a different, and cleaner, programming style 
â€¢	from traditional COM programming. You don't need to directly initialize COM, nor work 
â€¢	with COM pointers. 
â€¢	Nor do you need to handle HRESULT return codes. C++/WinRT converts error HRESULTs 
â€¢	to exceptions such as winrt::hresult-error for a natural and modern programming style. 
â€¢	For more info about error-handling, and code examples, see Error handling with 
â€¢	C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	Modify a Windows Desktop application project 
â€¢	to add C++/WinRT support 
â€¢	Some desktop projects (for example, the WinUI 3 templates in Visual Studio) have 
â€¢	C++/WinRT support built in. 
â€¢	But this section shows you how you can add C++/WinRT support to any Windows 
â€¢	Desktop application project that you might have. If you don't have an existing Windows 
â€¢	Desktop application project, then you can follow along with these steps by first creating 
â€¢	one. For example, open Visual Studio and create a Visual C++ > Windows Desktop > 
â€¢	Windows Desktop Application project. 
â€¢	You can optionally install the C++/WinRT Visual Studio Extension (VSIX) and the 
â€¢	NuGet package. For details, see Visual Studio support for C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	Set project properties 
â€¢	Go to project property General > Windows SDK Version, and select All Configurations 
â€¢	and All Platforms. Ensure that Windows SDK Version is set to 10.0.17134.0 (Windows 
â€¢	10, version 1803) or greater. 
â€¢	Confirm that you're not affected by Why won't my new project compile?. 
â€¢	Because C++/WinRT uses features from the C++17 standard, set project property 
â€¢	C/C++ > Language > C++ Language Standard to ISO C++17 Standard (/std:c++17).The precompiled header 
â€¢	The default project template creates a precompiled header for you, named either 
â€¢	framework.h , or stdafx.h . Rename that to pch.h . If you have a stdafx.cpp file, then 
â€¢	rename that to pch.cpp . Set project property C/C++ > Precompiled Headers > 
â€¢	Precompiled Header to Create (/Yc), and Precompiled Header File to pch.h. 
â€¢	Find and replace all #include "framework.h" (or #include "stdafx.h" ) with #include 
â€¢	"pch.h" . 
â€¢	In pch.h , include winrt/base.h . 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	// pch.h 
â€¢	... 
â€¢	#include <winrt/base.h> 
â€¢	Linking 
â€¢	The C++/WinRT language projection depends on certain Windows Runtime free (non
â€¢	member) functions, and entry points, that require linking to the WindowsApp.lib 
â€¢	umbrella library. This section describes three ways of satisfying the linker. 
â€¢	The first option is to add to your Visual Studio project all of the C++/WinRT MSBuild 
â€¢	properties and targets. To do this, install the Microsoft.Windows.CppWinRT NuGet 
â€¢	package into your project. Open the project in Visual Studio, click Project > Manage 
â€¢	NuGet Packages... > Browse, type or paste Microsoft.Windows.CppWinRT in the search 
â€¢	box, select the item in search results, and then click Install to install the package for that 
â€¢	project. 
â€¢	You can also use project link settings to explicitly link WindowsApp.lib . Or, you can do it 
â€¢	in source code (in pch.h , for example) like this. 
â€¢	C++/WinRT 
â€¢	#pragma comment(lib, "windowsapp") 
â€¢	You can now compile and link, and add C++/WinRT code to your project (for example, 
â€¢	code similar to that shown in the A C++/WinRT quick-start section, above). 
â€¢	The three main scenarios for C++/WinRTAs you use and become familiar with C++/WinRT, and work through the rest of the 
â€¢	documentation here, you'll likely notice that there are three main scenarios, as described 
â€¢	in the following sections. 
â€¢	Consuming Windows APIs and types 
â€¢	In other words, using, or calling APIs. For example, making API calls to communicate 
â€¢	using Bluetooth; to stream and present video; to integrate with the Windows shell; and 
â€¢	so on. C++/WinRT fully and uncompromisingly supports this category of scenario. For 
â€¢	more info, see Consume APIs with C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	Authoring Windows APIs and types 
â€¢	In other words, producing APIs and types. For example, producing the kinds of APIs 
â€¢	described in the section above; or the graphics APIs; the storage and file system APIs; 
â€¢	the networking APIs, and so on. For more info, see Author APIs with C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	Authoring APIs with C++/WinRT is a little more involved than consuming them, because 
â€¢	you must use IDL to define the shape of the API before you can implement it. There's a 
â€¢	walkthrough of doing that in XAML controls; bind to a C++/WinRT property. 
â€¢	XAML applications 
â€¢	This scenario is about building applications and controls on the XAML UI framework. 
â€¢	Working in a XAML application amounts to a combination of consuming and authoring. 
â€¢	But since XAML is the dominant UI framework on Windows today, and its influence over 
â€¢	the Windows Runtime is proportionate to that, it deserves its own category of scenario. 
â€¢	Be aware that XAML works best with programming languages that offer reflection. In 
â€¢	C++/WinRT, you sometimes have to do a little extra work in order to interoperate with 
â€¢	the XAML framework. All of those cases are covered in the documentation. Good places 
â€¢	to start are XAML controls; bind to a C++/WinRT property and XAML custom 
â€¢	(templated) controls with C++/WinRT. 
â€¢	Sample apps written in C++/WinRT 
â€¢	See Where can I find C++/WinRT sample apps?.

â€¢	Was this page helpful? 
â€¢	Provide product feedback 
â€¢	| Get help at Microsoft Q&A

â€¢	SyndicationFeed.Items property 
â€¢	winrt::hstring struct 
â€¢	winrt::hresult-error struct 
â€¢	C++/CX 
â€¢	Error handling with C++/WinRT 
â€¢	Interop between C++/WinRT and C++/CX 
â€¢	Interop between C++/WinRT and the ABI 
â€¢	Move to C++/WinRT from C++/CX 
â€¢	String handling in C++/WinRT 
â€¢	Related topics

â€¢	î£  NoFeedback
